  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

  <span id="freeText7209431873736087997" style="display:none">The Focus Student Editions are designed for Spanish language courses in literature and culture. Prepared with non-native Spanish speakers in mind, these editions include an introduction (in Spanish), the complete work, and linguistic and cultural notes in Spanish, a current bibliography and study questions.<br /><br />This Focus student edition is a Spanish drama of women in the villages of Spain. The play centers on the events of a house in Andalusia during a period of mourning, in which Bernarda Alba wields total control over her five daughters.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer601681913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4241670955752394898">La Casa De Bernarda Alba = The House of Bernarda Alba, Federico Garc√≠a Lorca <br/><br/><b>The House of Bernarda Alba</b> is a play by the Spanish dramatist <b>Federico Garc√≠a Lorca</b>. Commentators have often grouped it with Blood Wedding and Yerma as a <i>"rural trilogy"</i>. <b>Lorca</b> did not include it in his plan for a "trilogy of the Spanish land" (which remained unfinished at the time of his murder).<br/><br/>Upon her second husband's death, domineering matriarch Bernarda Alba imposes an eight-year mourning period on her household i</span>
  <span id="freeText4241670955752394898" style="display:none">La Casa De Bernarda Alba = The House of Bernarda Alba, Federico Garc√≠a Lorca <br /><br /><b>The House of Bernarda Alba</b> is a play by the Spanish dramatist <b>Federico Garc√≠a Lorca</b>. Commentators have often grouped it with Blood Wedding and Yerma as a <i>"rural trilogy"</i>. <b>Lorca</b> did not include it in his plan for a "trilogy of the Spanish land" (which remained unfinished at the time of his murder).<br /><br />Upon her second husband's death, domineering matriarch Bernarda Alba imposes an eight-year mourning period on her household in accordance with her family tradition. <br /><br />Bernarda has five daughters, aged between 20 and 39, whom she has controlled inexorably and prohibited from any form of relationship. The mourning period further isolates them and tension mounts within the household. <br /><br />After a mourning ritual at the family home, eldest daughter Angustias enters, having been absent while the guests were there. Bernarda fumes, assuming she had been listening to the men's conversation on the patio. <br /><br />Angustias inherited a large sum of money from her father, Bernarda's first husband, but Bernarda's second husband has left only small sums to his four daughters. Angustias' wealth attracts a young, attractive suitor from the village, Pepe el Romano. <br /><br />Her sisters are jealous, believing that it's unfair that plain, sickly Angustias should receive both the majority of the inheritance and the freedom to marry and escape their suffocating home environment. <br /><br />Youngest sister Adela, stricken with sudden spirit and jubilation after her father's funeral, defies her mother's orders and dons a green dress instead of remaining in mourning black. Her brief taste of youthful joy suddenly shatters when she discovers that Angustias will be marrying Pepe. Poncia, Bernarda's maid, advises Adela to bide her time: Angustias will probably die delivering her first child. Distressed, Adela threatens to run into the streets in her green dress, but her sisters manage to stop her. <br /><br />Suddenly they see Pepe coming down the street. She stays behind while her sisters rush to get a look, until a maid hints that she could get a better look from her bedroom window. As Poncia and Bernarda discuss the daughters' inheritances upstairs, Bernarda sees Angustias wearing makeup. Appalled that Angustias would defy her orders to remain in a state of mourning, Bernarda violently scrubs the makeup off her face. <br /><br />The other daughters enter, followed by Bernarda's elderly mother, Maria Josefa, who is usually locked away in her room. Maria Josefa announces that she wants to get married; she also warns Bernarda that she'll turn her daughters' hearts to dust if they cannot be free. Bernarda forces her back into her room. It turns out that Adela and Pepe are having a secret affair. <br /><br />Adela becomes increasingly volatile, defying her mother and quarreling with her sisters, particularly Martirio, who reveals her own feelings for Pepe. Adela shows the most horror when the family hears the latest gossip about how the townspeople recently tortured a young woman who had delivered and killed an illegitimate baby. <br /><br />Tension explodes as family members confront one another and Bernarda pursues Pepe with a gun. A gunshot is heard outside. Martirio and Bernarda return and imply that Pepe has been killed. Adela flees into another room. With Adela out of earshot, Martirio tells everyone else that Pepe actually fled on his pony. Bernarda remarks that as a woman she can't be blamed for poor aim. A shot is heard, immediately she calls for Adela, who has locked herself into a room. <br /><br />When Adela doesn't respond, Bernarda and Poncia force the door open. Soon Poncia's shriek is heard. She returns with her hands clasped around her neck and warns the family not to enter the room. Adela, not knowing that Pepe survived, has hanged herself. <br /><br />The closing lines of the play show Bernarda characteristically preoccupied with the family's reputation. She insists that Adela has died a virgin and demands that this be made known to the whole town. (The text implies that Adela and Pepe had an affair; Bernarda's moral code and pride keep this from registering). No one is to cry.<br /><br />ÿ™ÿßÿ±€åÿÆ ŸÜÿÆÿ≥ÿ™€åŸÜ ÿÆŸàÿßŸÜÿ¥: ÿ±Ÿàÿ≤ ŸÜŸáŸÖ ŸÖÿßŸá ŸÖÿßÿ±ÿ≥ ÿ≥ÿßŸÑ 1976 ŸÖ€åŸÑÿßÿØ€å<br /><br />ÿπŸÜŸàÿßŸÜ: ÿÆÿßŸÜŸá €å ÿ®ÿ±ŸÜÿßÿ±ÿØÿß ÿ¢ŸÑÿ®ÿßÿõ ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØŸá: ŸÅÿØÿ±€å⁄©Ÿà ⁄Øÿßÿ±ÿ≥€åÿß ŸÑŸàÿ±⁄©ÿßÿõ ŸÖÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖ: ŸÅ‚ÄçÿßŸÜ‚ÄçŸàÿ≥‚Äå ÿ®‚ÄçŸá‚ÄçÿßÿØÿ±ŸàŸÜ‚ÄçÿØÿõ ÿ™Ÿáÿ±ÿßŸÜÿå ŸÖ€åŸÜÿßÿå 1384ÿå ÿØÿ± 91ÿµÿõ ÿ¥ÿßÿ®⁄© 9646475701ÿõ ŸÖŸàÿ∂Ÿàÿπ ŸÜŸÖÿß€åÿ¥ŸÜÿßŸÖŸá Ÿáÿß€å ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØ⁄ØÿßŸÜ ÿßÿ≥ŸæÿßŸÜ€åÿß€å€å - ÿ≥ÿØŸá 20 ŸÖ<br /><br />ŸÖÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖ: ŸÖÿ≠ŸÖŸàÿØ ⁄©€åÿßŸÜŸàÿ¥ÿõ ÿ™Ÿáÿ±ÿßŸÜÿå ŸÜÿ¥ÿ± ŸÇÿ∑ÿ±Ÿáÿå 1388ÿå ÿØÿ± 103ÿµÿõ ÿ¥ÿßÿ®⁄©: 978964643419509ÿõ<br /><br />ÿß€åŸÜ ŸÅÿ±ÿßŸÖŸàÿ¥⁄©ÿßÿ± ŸÜŸÖÿß€åÿ¥ŸÜÿßŸÖŸá ÿ±ÿßÿå ÿØÿ± ⁄©ÿ™ÿßÿ®: ÿ≥Ÿá ŸÜŸÖÿß€åÿ¥ŸÜÿßŸÖŸáÿå ÿßÿ´ÿ±: ŸÑŸàÿ±⁄©ÿßÿå ÿ®ÿß ÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖŸá €å ÿ±ŸàÿßŸÜÿ¥ÿßÿØ ÿßÿ≠ŸÖÿØ ÿ¥ÿßŸÖŸÑŸàÿå ÿÆŸàÿßŸÜÿØŸá ÿßŸÖÿõ ŸÑŸàÿ±⁄©ÿß ÿß€åŸÜ ŸÜŸÖÿß€åÿ¥ŸÜÿßŸÖŸá ÿ±ÿß ÿØÿ± ÿπŸÜŸàÿßŸÜ ŸÅÿ±ÿπ€å ÿßÿ¥ ÿØÿ±ÿßŸÖÿß€å ÿ≤ŸÜÿßŸÜ ÿØÿ± ÿ±Ÿàÿ≥ÿ™ÿßŸáÿß€å ÿßÿ≥ŸæÿßŸÜ€åÿß ÿ™Ÿàÿµ€åŸÅ ⁄©ÿ±ÿØŸá ÿßÿ≥ÿ™ÿõ ÿß. ÿ¥ÿ±ÿ®€åÿßŸÜ€å</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14392325488278576799"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br/>4+ out of 5 stars to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22995.La_casa_de_Bernarda_Alba' title='La casa de Bernarda Alba by Federico Garc√≠a Lorca' rel='nofollow noopener'>La casa de Bernarda Alba</a>, a play written in 1936 but published in 1945 by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca' title='Federico Garc√≠a Lorca' rel='nofollow noopener'>Federico Garc√≠a Lorca</a> after his death. I adore this play for so many reasons. Originally written in Spanish, I read this as part of a course on literature as part of an English degree. I read it in English and watched two versions afterwards, and I will definitely say... reading it in Spanish was truly the best experience. The other great thing about this work is the ability to c</span>
  <span id="freeText14392325488278576799" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br />4+ out of 5 stars to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22995.La_casa_de_Bernarda_Alba" title="La casa de Bernarda Alba by Federico Garc√≠a Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">La casa de Bernarda Alba</a>, a play written in 1936 but published in 1945 by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca" title="Federico Garc√≠a Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Federico Garc√≠a Lorca</a> after his death. I adore this play for so many reasons. Originally written in Spanish, I read this as part of a course on literature as part of an English degree. I read it in English and watched two versions afterwards, and I will definitely say... reading it in Spanish was truly the best experience. The other great thing about this work is the ability to cover 5 different daughters, each with a unique personality, need and desire during their mourning period. La Mama will not let them do anything but sit in proper attire... but we all know that children do not listen to their mothers at various points in their life. Everyone needs to read this play simply to understand the beauty that is Lorca. All the plays deal with family and generational issues, gender and sexual revolutions (or sorts)... so much could be said about what Lorca was revealing from his own life! This is the kind of play that makes you love foreign or multi-cultural literature and stories. I remember being wow'd by the ability to convey such beauty in a way that I hadn't ever experienced, when it comes to another culture. I may have only been 18 or 19 at the time but it was quite profound. That said, it's a play. It's not that lengthy. And it's not a life-changer as far as coming to terms with something you are thinking about. It's more a wonderful cross-section of a culture, a time and a family who show you an alternate way of living. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>About Me</u>
  </i>

And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. Leave a comment and let me know what you think. Vote in the poll and ratings. Thanks for stopping by.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13088034425193687000">This is the second of Lorca‚Äôs ‚Äúrural trilogy‚Äù I have read, and if anything I liked it even more than <i>Bodas de Sangre</i>. In form and theme the two are quite similar. Like a Greek tragedy, the plot is simplicity itself, with one obvious conflict and one calamitous resolution. Again, Lorca‚Äôs power as a dramatist comes, not from subtlety or wit, but from pure passion. The incompatibility between traditional values and human impulses, with all its tragic implications, is laid bare by Lorca, who shows u</span>
  <span id="freeText13088034425193687000" style="display:none">This is the second of Lorca‚Äôs ‚Äúrural trilogy‚Äù I have read, and if anything I liked it even more than <i>Bodas de Sangre</i>. In form and theme the two are quite similar. Like a Greek tragedy, the plot is simplicity itself, with one obvious conflict and one calamitous resolution. Again, Lorca‚Äôs power as a dramatist comes, not from subtlety or wit, but from pure passion. The incompatibility between traditional values and human impulses, with all its tragic implications, is laid bare by Lorca, who shows us a culture whose religious mores and gender norms oppress women and deprive them of a fulfilling life. Strikingly, the cast of characters is entirely female, even though the conflict revolves around a male who is always offstage. This allows Lorca to focus on a side of life that was often swept aside, while maintaining an atmosphere of tension and constraint that makes the play so riveting. I am excited for <i>Yerma</i>. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6761360538436164514">3/5<br/><br/>Altogether I liked this play. I wasn't entirely moved by it, but I think I might prefer to see it performed live. On paper, several of the main female characters blended together, which muddled up their motivations and personalities, but I think on stage the various actors' choices may lend themselves to create more distinct individuals. Ultimately it just made me sad that these women all shared the same obsession over this mystery man who, to be honest, doesn't sound all that great. This obs</span>
  <span id="freeText6761360538436164514" style="display:none">3/5<br /><br />Altogether I liked this play. I wasn't entirely moved by it, but I think I might prefer to see it performed live. On paper, several of the main female characters blended together, which muddled up their motivations and personalities, but I think on stage the various actors' choices may lend themselves to create more distinct individuals. Ultimately it just made me sad that these women all shared the same obsession over this mystery man who, to be honest, doesn't sound all that great. This obsession ultimately led to the dissolution and demise of the family, and basically NO characters get any sort of redemption. However, I do love a good dramatic play, and this one hit all the points: lies, deception, death, mourning, family, and a secret love affair.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6567770282212217087">Coincidentally, another play featuring the fortunes of a band of desperate upper-class sisters after their father dies. (When you're looking for widely available plays with lots of roles for women, this sort of thing comes up a lot.)<br/><br/>But where Chekhov's <i>The Three Sisters</i> is dry comedy and disappointed romance, Federico Garcia Lorca's <i>The House of Bernarda Alba</i> is hot, repressed passion and the capital-D Drama.<br/><br/>Honestly, the role of the tyrannical matriarch Bernarda is a steal. And then there's the</span>
  <span id="freeText6567770282212217087" style="display:none">Coincidentally, another play featuring the fortunes of a band of desperate upper-class sisters after their father dies. (When you're looking for widely available plays with lots of roles for women, this sort of thing comes up a lot.)<br /><br />But where Chekhov's <i>The Three Sisters</i> is dry comedy and disappointed romance, Federico Garcia Lorca's <i>The House of Bernarda Alba</i> is hot, repressed passion and the capital-D Drama.<br /><br />Honestly, the role of the tyrannical matriarch Bernarda is a steal. And then there's the horny, senile grandmother Maria Josefa, and the sly housekeeper Poncia. The daughters are less exciting overall, though snarky and hunchbacked Martirio is an exception. Does the play get too dramatic at the end? Perhaps, but it must be a hoot to play. Rating: 3.5 stars</span>

<div id="review_1488648361" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1488648361" />
    <a title="Jack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5400547-jack"><img alt="Jack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536008080p2/5400547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1488648361?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack" href="/user/show/5400547-jack">Jack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789505811052" href="/book/show/818733.La_Casa_de_Bernarda_Alba">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5400547-jack?shelf=hispanic-literature">hispanic-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1488648361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12599248185164669623">4/5<br/>A tragedy in which the reader enters Lorca's wounded soul. <br/><br/>This was the final play Lorca wrote before he was captured and killed by fascists in 1936, at the start of the Spanish Civil War. It deals with the titular character (Bernarda) controlling her five daughters by locking them up in her Andalusian house after her second husband has died. Bernarda seems to believe that keeping the daughters away from society constitutes a period of morning. As tensions start rising and the women become mo</span>
  <span id="freeText12599248185164669623" style="display:none">4/5<br />A tragedy in which the reader enters Lorca's wounded soul. <br /><br />This was the final play Lorca wrote before he was captured and killed by fascists in 1936, at the start of the Spanish Civil War. It deals with the titular character (Bernarda) controlling her five daughters by locking them up in her Andalusian house after her second husband has died. Bernarda seems to believe that keeping the daughters away from society constitutes a period of morning. As tensions start rising and the women become more and more oppressed, a love interest, Pepe el Romano, appears. Bernarda can oppress everything but the feelings in her daughters' hearts.<br /><br />Overall this was a quick, enjoyable play to read with only 3 acts in total. The characterisation is very precise and each character has a distinct approach to their situation. Bernarda's relentless, barbaric control over her daughters was shocking - she is so bitter and twisted in her approach to them. Though I liked reading about a villainous female character for a change, it's not something I've seen much of in the Civil-War narratives I've studied. Bernarda also has a vile attitude towards her servant (La Poncia) and her mother (Mar√≠a Josefa), who she treats as being insignificant. Obviously the themes of oppression and family relationships were pretty much constantly presented throughout the entire play, but I was also interested in the themes of death, madness, marginalisation, and marriage. These are all presented in the context of 1930s Spain. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12599248185164669623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15147608787446026412">Read for college. This is the second play I've read by Lorca and I enjoyed it even more than <i>Yerma</i>, his other play I've read. <i>La casa de Bernarda Alba</i> offers an insight into what society in the 20th century in Spain was like, especially for women. We can especially see the oppression they went through - imposed both by society itself and also by their mothers. This is an aspect I usually appreciate in books: they can be the best way of understanding and studying the past, which is crucial for hu</span>
  <span id="freeText15147608787446026412" style="display:none">Read for college. This is the second play I've read by Lorca and I enjoyed it even more than <i>Yerma</i>, his other play I've read. <i>La casa de Bernarda Alba</i> offers an insight into what society in the 20th century in Spain was like, especially for women. We can especially see the oppression they went through - imposed both by society itself and also by their mothers. This is an aspect I usually appreciate in books: they can be the best way of understanding and studying the past, which is crucial for humans to learn how to better in the present.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153253187" />
    <a title="Meagan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3870442-meagan"><img alt="Meagan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338947247p2/3870442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153253187?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meagan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meagan" href="/user/show/3870442-meagan">Meagan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789505811052" href="/book/show/818733.La_Casa_de_Bernarda_Alba">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3870442-meagan?shelf=2011-books">2011-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153253187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1266983776792172434">I wasn't expecting to like this play. We read it in my Spanish literature class, and I was dreading it the entire time. However, Lorca is surprisingly funny, and he writes with a depth and feeling that is incredible. I read the original Spanish version, so I can't comment on any English translation, but if you can read Spanish, this play, although somewhat depressing, is completely memorable. It is truly the star in Lorca's crown. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8803475950056424723">Outstanding use of symbolism once again by Lorca. In less than 100 pages he addresses all manner of social, political, cultural and class issues as well as issues of sex and gender. Wrote about this play in my exam and didn't even have to stop to think, there is so much to say and every line is laden with meaning.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_328526458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/328526458" />
    <a title="Cristobo De" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8376419-cristobo-de"><img alt="Cristobo De" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/328526458?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristobo De" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristobo De" href="/user/show/8376419-cristobo-de">Cristobo De</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer328526458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6474704376666665725">I didn`t read the book, I didn`t watch the play: there is a movie directed by Mario Camus, which I enjoyed greatly, and judging by other reviews I think I haven`t missed much from the plot. <br/>I came to write this because I am puzzled that so many people regard this as a "feminist play" or as an "allegory of Franco`s dictatorship". Amazing: to begin with, seems like the play was written in 1936, BEFORE Franco came to power. God knows Spain was not the perfect democracy before Franco, but there was </span>
  <span id="freeText6474704376666665725" style="display:none">I didn`t read the book, I didn`t watch the play: there is a movie directed by Mario Camus, which I enjoyed greatly, and judging by other reviews I think I haven`t missed much from the plot. <br />I came to write this because I am puzzled that so many people regard this as a "feminist play" or as an "allegory of Franco`s dictatorship". Amazing: to begin with, seems like the play was written in 1936, BEFORE Franco came to power. God knows Spain was not the perfect democracy before Franco, but there was nothing in Spanish history which could anticipate the horrors of his regime.<br /> Then, as to the "feminist" thing, I just don`t get it. What I saw was a bunch of women who work hard to waste their own lives and ruin the lives of those near them. The key factor in Bernarda`s environment is gossip. Gossip is her only concern, and she locks away her daughters, nearly buries them alive, just so that women in town can`t find any flaw in their behaviour. Sorry to say, gals, but this all sounds quite mysoginistic to me. Isn`t all this gossiping and meanness a classic mysoginistic cliche?<br />I don`t quite like Lorca very much but this one I enjoyed. Very well written, the man manages to keep interest all along even though no much happens. I think the reader / watcher simply enjoys the trip into such a strange, alien society and mindset. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6474704376666665725" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_644712432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/644712432" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21155710-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517767451p2/21155710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/644712432?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/21155710-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789721037168" href="/book/show/17287339-a-casa-de-bernarda-alba">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21155710-maria?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21155710-maria?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21155710-maria?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer644712432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11584291675669693158">Okay, I just loved this book!<br/>Not only it is a very small book, it is a play, so it's read very quickly. And because it is a play, we can't judge it the same way we judge a novel, right?<br/>It's about family dysfunction, hypocrisy, passion, jealousy, resentment and the role of women in society.<br/>Bernarda is a shrewd, smart, unreliant, violent, skeptic, pragmatic, selfish, controlling woman. She worships tradition, honor and calmness. She is a dictator. And she locks her five daughters inside their own </span>
  <span id="freeText11584291675669693158" style="display:none">Okay, I just loved this book!<br />Not only it is a very small book, it is a play, so it's read very quickly. And because it is a play, we can't judge it the same way we judge a novel, right?<br />It's about family dysfunction, hypocrisy, passion, jealousy, resentment and the role of women in society.<br />Bernarda is a shrewd, smart, unreliant, violent, skeptic, pragmatic, selfish, controlling woman. She worships tradition, honor and calmness. She is a dictator. And she locks her five daughters inside their own house, having them lead a boring life in order to keep the neighbor‚Äôs (which she despises) nose out of their lives (since although she breathes gossips she would never want to hear any concerning her or her daughters), ergo, just to keep up appearances.<br />Angustias, the oldest daughter, is allowed to meet with a man through her bedroom's window, and her bedroom's window only. But he is the only man ever around; now you can imagine the consequences of having only one man and so many women! Forced to live like this, some of them actually ended up falling in love with the same man, who, although had proposed to the oldest, was cheating on her with the youngest (who was particularly rebelled against her mother's regime).<br />The ending is strong and tragic and portrays society beautifully.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11584291675669693158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_414766488" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414766488" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11318725-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472084226p2/11318725.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/414766488?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/11318725-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822216537" href="/book/show/867666.The_House_of_Bernarda_Alba">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer414766488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8464074175484968973">I'll never forget my first experience with this shattering drama. I was in junior high school, spending the night at a friend's house, and this play was on television. After the play, an actor recited Garcia Lorca's poem "Five in the Afternoon." I didn't understand the play at first, but I read it when I was in college and kept on reading Garcia Lorca. He has a dark vision of women's lives. The plays are difficult to engage with, but well worth the effort. He's a genius.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UWaWjy9mkQkowYVKNXWdTOX9TU6foVeKcBeAiBSgpL8xpzwM5yd8U06DQP37o7YvF5ZCg4TqRwLZtFlMmGoEhQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AxmqoULkyp3dvDj4cspXkdl1XlTUvcqa5PiDkwz+AQdj2AAiiqUnx7v+/U+8HHzyKx5Rmc/22hJNW1pXgDShPQ==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer6320370172676704531">Born in Fuente Vaqueros, Granada, Spain, June 5 1898; died near Granada, August 19 1936, Garc√≠a Lorca is one of Spain's most deeply appreciated and highly revered poets and dramatists. His murder by the Nationalists at the start of the Spanish civil war brought sudden international fame, accompanied by an excess of political rhetoric which led a later generation to question his merits; after the i</span>
  <span id="freeText6320370172676704531" style="display:none">Born in Fuente Vaqueros, Granada, Spain, June 5 1898; died near Granada, August 19 1936, Garc√≠a Lorca is one of Spain's most deeply appreciated and highly revered poets and dramatists. His murder by the Nationalists at the start of the Spanish civil war brought sudden international fame, accompanied by an excess of political rhetoric which led a later generation to question his merits; after the inevitable slump, his reputation has recovered (largely with a shift in interest to the less obvious works). He must now be bracketed with Machado as one of the two greatest poets Spain has produced in the 20th century, and he is certainly Spain's greatest dramatist since the Golden Age. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6320370172676704531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
